<p><b>American English</b> (<b>AmE</b>, <b>AE</b>, <b>AmEng</b>, <b>USEng</b>, <b>en-US</b>),<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-3"><a href="#cite_note-3">[3]</a></sup> sometimes called <b>United States English</b> or <b>U.S. English</b>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-4"><a href="#cite_note-4">[4]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-5"><a href="#cite_note-5">[5]</a></sup> is the set of varieties of the <a href="/wiki/English_language" title="English language">English language</a> native to the United States.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-6"><a href="#cite_note-6">[6]</a></sup>
</p><p>English is the most widely spoken language in the United States and is the common language used by the federal government, to the extent that all laws and compulsory education are practiced in English. Although not an officially established language of the whole country, English is considered the <i><a href="/wiki/De_facto" title="De facto">de facto</a></i> language and is given official status by 32 of the 50 state governments.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-7"><a href="#cite_note-7">[7]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-8"><a href="#cite_note-8">[8]</a></sup> As an example, while both Spanish and English have equivalent status in the local courts of <a href="/wiki/Puerto_Rico" title="Puerto Rico">Puerto Rico</a>, under federal law, English is the official language for any matters being referred to the <a href="/wiki/United_States_district_court" title="United States district court">United States district court</a> for the territory.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-9"><a href="#cite_note-9">[9]</a></sup>
</p><p>The use of English in the United States is a result of <a href="/wiki/British_colonization_of_the_Americas" title="British colonization of the Americas">English and British colonization of the Americas</a>. The first wave of English-speaking settlers arrived in North America during the 17th century, followed by further migrations in the 18th and 19th centuries. Since then, American English has developed into new dialects, in some cases under the influence of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/West_African_languages" title="West African languages">West African</a> and <a href="/wiki/Indigenous_languages_of_the_Americas" title="Indigenous languages of the Americas">Native American languages</a>, <a href="/wiki/German_language" title="German language">German</a>, <a href="/wiki/Dutch_language" title="Dutch language">Dutch</a>, <a href="/wiki/Irish_language" title="Irish language">Irish</a>, <a href="/wiki/Spanish_language" title="Spanish language">Spanish</a>, and other languages of successive waves of immigrants to the United States.
</p><p>American English varieties form a linguistic continuum of dialects more similar to each other than to English dialects of other countries, including some common pronunciations and other features found nationwide.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-10"><a href="#cite_note-10">[10]</a></sup> Any <a href="/wiki/North_American_English" title="North American English">North American English</a> <a href="/wiki/Accent_(sociolinguistics)" title="Accent (sociolinguistics)">accent</a> perceived as free of noticeably local, ethnic, or cultural <a href="/wiki/Markedness" title="Markedness">markers</a> is popularly called <a href="/wiki/General_American" title="General American">"General" or "Standard" American</a>, a fairly uniform standard of broadcast mass media and the highly educated. Otherwise, according to Labov, with the major exception of <a href="/wiki/Southern_American_English" title="Southern American English">Southern American English</a>, regional accents throughout the country are not yielding to this standard,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-11"><a href="#cite_note-11">[11]</a></sup> and historical and present linguistic evidence does not support the notion of there being one single "mainstream" American accent.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-12"><a href="#cite_note-12">[12]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-13"><a href="#cite_note-13">[13]</a></sup> On the contrary, the sound of American English continues to evolve, with some local accents disappearing, but several larger regional accents emerging.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-PBS_14-0"><a href="#cite_note-PBS-14">[14]</a></sup>
</p>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Varieties">Varieties</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=American_English&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: Varieties">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main articles: <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Regional_vocabularies_of_American_English" title="Regional vocabularies of American English">Regional vocabularies of American English</a> and <a href="/wiki/North_American_English_regional_phonology" title="North American English regional phonology">North American English regional phonology</a></div>
<p>While written American English is largely standardized across the country, there are several recognizable variations in the spoken language, both in pronunciation and in vernacular vocabulary. The regional sounds of present-day American English are reportedly engaged in a complex phenomenon of "both convergence and divergence": some accents are homogenizing and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Accent_levelling" title="Accent levelling">levelling</a>, while others are diversifying and deviating further away from one another.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-15"><a href="#cite_note-15">[15]</a></sup> In 2010, <a href="/wiki/William_Labov" title="William Labov">William Labov</a> summarized the current state of regional American accents as follows:<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Labov_2010_16-0"><a href="#cite_note-Labov_2010-16">[16]</a></sup>
</p><p>Some regional American English has undergone "vigorous new sound changes" since the mid-nineteenth century onwards, spawning relatively recent <a href="/wiki/Mid-Atlantic_American_English" title="Mid-Atlantic American English">Mid-Atlantic</a> (centered on <a href="/wiki/Philadelphia" title="Philadelphia">Philadelphia</a> and <a href="/wiki/Baltimore" title="Baltimore">Baltimore</a>), <a href="/wiki/Western_Pennsylvania_English" title="Western Pennsylvania English">Western Pennsylvania</a> (centered on <a href="/wiki/Pittsburgh" title="Pittsburgh">Pittsburgh</a>), <a href="/wiki/Inland_Northern_American_English" title="Inland Northern American English">Inland Northern</a> (centered on <a href="/wiki/Chicago" title="Chicago">Chicago</a>, <a href="/wiki/Detroit" title="Detroit">Detroit</a>, and the <a href="/wiki/Great_Lakes_region" title="Great Lakes region">Great Lakes region</a>), <a href="/wiki/Midland_American_English" title="Midland American English">Midland</a> (centered on <a href="/wiki/Indianapolis" title="Indianapolis">Indianapolis</a>, <a href="/wiki/Columbus,_Ohio" title="Columbus, Ohio">Columbus</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Kansas_City,_Missouri" title="Kansas City, Missouri">Kansas City</a>) and <a href="/wiki/Western_American_English" title="Western American English">Western regional accents</a>, all of which "are now more different from each other than they were fifty or a hundred years ago". Meanwhile, distinguishing features of the <a href="/wiki/Eastern_New_England_English" title="Eastern New England English">Eastern New England</a> (centered on <a href="/wiki/Boston" title="Boston">Boston</a>) and <a href="/wiki/New_York_City_English" title="New York City English">New York City accents</a> appear to be stable. "On the other hand, dialects of many smaller cities have receded in favor of the new regional patterns";<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Labov_2010_16-1"><a href="#cite_note-Labov_2010-16">[16]</a></sup> for example, the traditional accents of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Charleston,_SC" title="Charleston, SC">Charleston</a> and of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Cincinnati,_OH" title="Cincinnati, OH">Cincinnati</a> have given way to the Midland regional accent, and of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/St._Louis,_MO" title="St. Louis, MO">St. Louis</a> now approaches the sounds of an Inland Northern or Midland accent. At the same time, the <a href="/wiki/Southern_American_English" title="Southern American English">Southern regional accent</a>, despite the huge population it covers,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-PBS_14-1"><a href="#cite_note-PBS-14">[14]</a></sup> "is on the whole slowly receding due to cultural stigma: younger speakers everywhere in the South are shifting away from the <a href="/wiki/Markedness" title="Markedness">marked</a> features of Southern speech." Finally, the <a href="/wiki/High_Tider" title="High Tider">"Hoi Toider" dialect</a> shows the paradox of receding among younger speakers in the islands of North Carolina's <a href="/wiki/Outer_Banks" title="Outer Banks">Outer Banks</a>, yet strengthening in the islands of the <a href="/wiki/Chesapeake_Bay" title="Chesapeake Bay">Chesapeake Bay</a>.
</p>
<div style="text-align:center; background:#bfd7ff; width:75%"><b>Major regional dialects of American English</b></div>

<div style="position:absolute; font-size:85%; left:48px; top:169px"><a href="/wiki/California_English" title="California English"><span style="color:green;">California</span></a></div>
<div style="position:absolute; font-size:85%; left:414px; top:160px"><a href="/wiki/Appalachian_English" title="Appalachian English"><span style="color:fuchsia">Appalachia</span></a></div>
<div style="position:absolute; font-size:85%; left:552px; top:85px"><a href="/wiki/Boston_accent" title="Boston accent"><span style="color:teal">Boston</span></a></div>
<div style="position:absolute; font-size:85%; left:540px; top:94px"><a href="/wiki/Eastern_New_England_English#Southeastern_New_England" title="Eastern New England English"><span style="color:blue">Rhode Island</span></a></div>
<div style="position:absolute; font-size:85%; left:21px; top:42.4px"><a href="/wiki/Pacific_Northwest_English" title="Pacific Northwest English"><span style="color:olive">Pacific Northwest</span></a></div>
<div style="position:absolute; font-size:85%; left:501px; top:160px"><a href="/wiki/High_Tider" title="High Tider"><span style="color:hotpink">Chesapeake &amp;<br/> Outer Banks</span></a></div>
<div style="position:absolute; font-size:85%; left:552px; top:55px"><a href="/wiki/Maine_accent" title="Maine accent"><span style="color:teal">Maine</span></a></div>
<div style="position:absolute; font-size:85%; left:354px; top:242.8px"><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Yat_dialect" title="Yat dialect"><span style="color:navy">New <br/>Orleans</span></a></div>
<div style="position:absolute; font-size:85%; left:360px; top:28px"><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Yooper_dialect" title="Yooper dialect"><span style="color:#964B00">Upper <br/>Peninsula</span></a></div>
<div style="position:absolute; font-size:85%; left:504px; top:133px"><a href="/wiki/Philadelphia_English" title="Philadelphia English"><span style="color:purple">Philadelphia</span></a> <span style="color:purple">&amp;</span> <br/><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Baltimore_English" title="Baltimore English"><span style="color:purple">Baltimore</span></a></div>
</div> The map above shows the major regional dialects of American English (in <a href="/wiki/All_caps" title="All caps">all caps</a>) plus smaller and more local dialects, as demarcated primarily by Labov et al.'s <i><a href="/wiki/The_Atlas_of_North_American_English" title="The Atlas of North American English">The Atlas of North American English</a></i>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-17"><a href="#cite_note-17">[17]</a></sup> as well as the related Telsur Project's <a class="external text" href="http://www.ling.upenn.edu/phono_atlas/home.html#regional" rel="nofollow">regional maps</a>. Any region may also contain speakers of a "<a href="/wiki/General_American" title="General American">General American</a>" accent that resists the marked features of their region. Furthermore, this map does not account for speakers of ethnic or cultural varieties (such as <a href="/wiki/African-American_English" title="African-American English">African-American English</a>, <a href="/wiki/Chicano_English" title="Chicano English">Chicano English</a>, <a href="/wiki/Cajun_English" title="Cajun English">Cajun English</a>, etc.). All regional American English, unless specifically stated otherwise, can be assumed to be <a href="/wiki/Rhoticity_in_English" title="Rhoticity in English">rhotic</a>, with the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Father%E2%80%93bother_merger" title="Father–bother merger"><i>father–bother</i> merger</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mary%E2%80%93marry%E2%80%93merry_merger" title="Mary–marry–merry merger"><i>Mary–marry–merry</i> merger</a>, and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki//%C3%A6/_tensing" title="/æ/ tensing">pre-nasal "short <i>a</i>" tensing</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-18"><a href="#cite_note-18">[note 1]</a></sup>
<p>
</p>
</td>
<td style="width: 50%;text-align: left; vertical-align: top;">
<ul>
<li><div class="mw-collapsible mw-collapsed" style="box-sizing:border-box;width:100%;font-size:95%;padding:4px;border:none;"><div style="font-size:100%;line-height:1.6;font-weight:bold;background:transparent;text-align:center;;text-align:left"><a href="/wiki/Western_American_English" title="Western American English">Western</a></div><div class="mw-collapsible-content" style="background:transparent;text-align:left;;">
<p>The Western dialect, including Californian and New Mexican sub-types (with Pacific Northwest English also, arguably, a sub-type), is defined by:
</p>
<ul><li><i>Cot–caught</i> merger to <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">[<a href="/wiki/Open_back_unrounded_vowel" title="Open back unrounded vowel">ɑ</a>]</span><small class="nowrap"> (<span class="unicode haudio"><span class="fn"><span style="white-space:nowrap"><a href="/wiki/File:Open_back_unrounded_vowel.ogg" title="About this sound"><img alt="About this sound" data-file-height="20" data-file-width="20" height="11" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8a/Loudspeaker.svg/11px-Loudspeaker.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8a/Loudspeaker.svg/17px-Loudspeaker.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8a/Loudspeaker.svg/22px-Loudspeaker.svg.png 2x" width="11"/></a> </span><a class="internal" href="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/e/e5/Open_back_unrounded_vowel.ogg" title="Open back unrounded vowel.ogg">listen</a></span></span>)</small></li>
<li><span class="nowrap"><span class="IPA nopopups noexcerpt"><a href="/wiki/Help:IPA/English" title="Help:IPA/English">/<span style="border-bottom:1px dotted"><span title="/oʊ/: 'o' in 'code'">oʊ</span></span>/</a></span></span> is <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">[oʊ]</span></li>
<li><span class="nowrap"><span class="IPA nopopups noexcerpt"><a href="/wiki/Help:IPA/English" title="Help:IPA/English">/<span style="border-bottom:1px dotted"><span title="/uː/: 'oo' in 'goose'">uː</span></span>/</a></span></span> is <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">[ü~ʉ]</span></li></ul>
</div></div></li>
<li><div class="mw-collapsible mw-collapsed" style="box-sizing:border-box;width:100%;font-size:95%;padding:4px;border:none;"><div style="font-size:100%;line-height:1.6;font-weight:bold;background:transparent;text-align:center;;text-align:left"><a href="/wiki/North-Central_American_English" title="North-Central American English">North Central</a></div><div class="mw-collapsible-content" style="background:transparent;text-align:left;;">
<p>The North Central ("Upper Midwest") dialect, including an Upper Michigan sub-type, is defined by:
</p>
<ul><li><i>Cot–caught</i> merger to <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">[<a href="/wiki/Open_central_unrounded_vowel" title="Open central unrounded vowel">ä</a>]</span><small class="nowrap"> (<span class="unicode haudio"><span class="fn"><span style="white-space:nowrap"><a href="/wiki/File:Open_central_unrounded_vowel.ogg" title="About this sound"><img alt="About this sound" data-file-height="20" data-file-width="20" height="11" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8a/Loudspeaker.svg/11px-Loudspeaker.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8a/Loudspeaker.svg/17px-Loudspeaker.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8a/Loudspeaker.svg/22px-Loudspeaker.svg.png 2x" width="11"/></a> </span><a class="internal" href="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/5/50/Open_central_unrounded_vowel.ogg" title="Open central unrounded vowel.ogg">listen</a></span></span>)</small><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Labov_2006_141_19-0"><a href="#cite_note-Labov_2006_141-19">[18]</a></sup></li>
<li><span class="nowrap"><span class="IPA nopopups noexcerpt"><a href="/wiki/Help:IPA/English" title="Help:IPA/English">/<span style="border-bottom:1px dotted"><span title="/oʊ/: 'o' in 'code'">oʊ</span></span>/</a></span></span> is <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">[oʊ]</span> (and may even <a href="/wiki/Monophthong" title="Monophthong">monophthongize</a> to <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">[o]</span>)<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Labov_2006_141_19-1"><a href="#cite_note-Labov_2006_141-19">[18]</a></sup></li>
<li><span class="nowrap"><span class="IPA nopopups noexcerpt"><a href="/wiki/Help:IPA/English" title="Help:IPA/English">/<span style="border-bottom:1px dotted"><span title="/uː/: 'oo' in 'goose'">uː</span></span>/</a></span></span> is <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">[u]</span><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Labov_2006_141_19-2"><a href="#cite_note-Labov_2006_141-19">[18]</a></sup></li></ul>
</div></div></li>
<li><div class="mw-collapsible mw-collapsed" style="box-sizing:border-box;width:100%;font-size:95%;padding:4px;border:none;"><div style="font-size:100%;line-height:1.6;font-weight:bold;background:transparent;text-align:center;;text-align:left"><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Inland_North" title="Inland North">Inland Northern</a></div><div class="mw-collapsible-content" style="background:transparent;text-align:left;;">
<p>The Inland Northern ("Great Lakes") dialect, including its less advanced Western New England (WNE) sub-types, is defined by:
</p>
<ul><li>No <i>cot–caught</i> merger: the <i>cot</i> vowel is <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">[ä~a]</span> and <i>caught</i> vowel is <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">[ɒ]</span></li>
<li><span class="nowrap"><span class="IPA nopopups noexcerpt"><a href="/wiki/Help:IPA/English" title="Help:IPA/English">/<span style="border-bottom:1px dotted"><span title="/æ/: 'a' in 'bad'">æ</span></span>/</a></span></span> is universally <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">[ɛə]</span>, the triggering event for the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Northern_Cities_Vowel_Shift" title="Northern Cities Vowel Shift">Northern Cities Vowel Shift</a> in more advanced sub-types (<span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">[ɛə]</span> ← <span class="nowrap"><span class="IPA nopopups noexcerpt"><a href="/wiki/Help:IPA/English" title="Help:IPA/English">/<span style="border-bottom:1px dotted"><span title="/æ/: 'a' in 'bad'">æ</span></span>/</a></span></span> ← <span class="nowrap"><span class="IPA nopopups noexcerpt"><a href="/wiki/Help:IPA/English" title="Help:IPA/English">/<span style="border-bottom:1px dotted"><span title="/ɑː/: 'a' in 'father'">ɑː</span></span>/</a></span></span>~<span class="nowrap"><span class="IPA nopopups noexcerpt"><a href="/wiki/Help:IPA/English" title="Help:IPA/English">/<span style="border-bottom:1px dotted"><span title="/ɒ/: 'o' in 'body'">ɒ</span></span>/</a></span></span>  ←  <span class="nowrap"><span class="IPA nopopups noexcerpt"><a href="/wiki/Help:IPA/English" title="Help:IPA/English">/<span style="border-bottom:1px dotted"><span title="/ɔː/: 'au' in 'fraud'">ɔː</span></span>/</a></span></span> ← <span class="nowrap"><span class="IPA nopopups noexcerpt"><a href="/wiki/Help:IPA/English" title="Help:IPA/English">/<span style="border-bottom:1px dotted"><span title="/ʌ/: 'u' in 'cut'">ʌ</span></span>/</a></span></span> ← <span class="nowrap"><span class="IPA nopopups noexcerpt"><a href="/wiki/Help:IPA/English" title="Help:IPA/English">/<span style="border-bottom:1px dotted"><span title="/ɛ/: 'e' in 'dress'">ɛ</span></span>/</a></span></span>)<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-20"><a href="#cite_note-20">[19]</a></sup></li>
<li><span class="nowrap"><span class="IPA nopopups noexcerpt"><a href="/wiki/Help:IPA/English" title="Help:IPA/English">/<span style="border-bottom:1px dotted"><span title="/oʊ/: 'o' in 'code'">oʊ</span></span>/</a></span></span> is <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">[oʊ~ʌo]</span></li></ul>
</div></div></li>
<li><div class="mw-collapsible mw-collapsed" style="box-sizing:border-box;width:100%;font-size:95%;padding:4px;border:none;"><div style="font-size:100%;line-height:1.6;font-weight:bold;background:transparent;text-align:center;;text-align:left"><a href="/wiki/Midland_American_English" title="Midland American English">Midland</a></div><div class="mw-collapsible-content" style="background:transparent;text-align:left;;">
<p>The Midland dialect is defined by:
</p>
<ul><li><i>Cot–caught</i> merger is in transition<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-21"><a href="#cite_note-21">[20]</a></sup></li>
<li><span class="nowrap"><span class="IPA nopopups noexcerpt"><a href="/wiki/Help:IPA/English" title="Help:IPA/English">/<span style="border-bottom:1px dotted"><span title="/aɪ/: 'i' in 'tide'">aɪ</span></span>/</a></span></span> may be <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">[aː]</span>, often only before <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">/l/</span>, <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">/m/</span>, <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">/n/</span>, or <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">/ɹ/</span></li>
<li><span class="nowrap"><span class="IPA nopopups noexcerpt"><a href="/wiki/Help:IPA/English" title="Help:IPA/English">/<span style="border-bottom:1px dotted"><span title="/aʊ/: 'ou' in 'mouth'">aʊ</span></span>/</a></span></span> is <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">[æɵ~æo]</span><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Labov_2006_237_22-0"><a href="#cite_note-Labov_2006_237-22">[21]</a></sup></li>
<li><span class="nowrap"><span class="IPA nopopups noexcerpt"><a href="/wiki/Help:IPA/English" title="Help:IPA/English">/<span style="border-bottom:1px dotted"><span title="/oʊ/: 'o' in 'code'">oʊ</span></span>/</a></span></span> is <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">[ɜʊ~ɵʊ]</span></li></ul>
</div></div></li>
<li><div class="mw-collapsible mw-collapsed" style="box-sizing:border-box;width:100%;font-size:95%;padding:4px;border:none;"><div style="font-size:100%;line-height:1.6;font-weight:bold;background:transparent;text-align:center;;text-align:left"><a href="/wiki/Western_Pennsylvania_English" title="Western Pennsylvania English">WPA</a></div><div class="mw-collapsible-content" style="background:transparent;text-align:left;;">
<p>The Western Pennsylvania dialect, including its advanced Pittsburgh sub-type, is defined by:
</p>
<ul><li><i>Cot–caught</i> merger to <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">[ɒ~ɔ]</span>, the triggering event for the Pittsburgh Chain Shift in the city itself (<span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">[ɒ~ɔ]</span> ← <span class="nowrap"><span class="IPA nopopups noexcerpt"><a href="/wiki/Help:IPA/English" title="Help:IPA/English">/<span style="border-bottom:1px dotted"><span title="/ɑː/: 'a' in 'father'">ɑː</span></span>/</a></span></span> ← <span class="nowrap"><span class="IPA nopopups noexcerpt"><a href="/wiki/Help:IPA/English" title="Help:IPA/English">/<span style="border-bottom:1px dotted"><span title="/ʌ/: 'u' in 'cut'">ʌ</span></span>/</a></span></span>) but no trace of the Canadian Shift<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-23"><a href="#cite_note-23">[22]</a></sup></li>
<li><span class="nowrap"><span class="IPA nopopups noexcerpt"><a href="/wiki/Help:IPA/English" title="Help:IPA/English">/<span style="border-bottom:1px dotted"><span title="/oʊ/: 'o' in 'code'">oʊ</span></span>/</a></span></span> is <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">[ɜʊ~ɞʊ]</span><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-24"><a href="#cite_note-24">[23]</a></sup></li>
<li><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Full%E2%80%93fool_merger" title="Full–fool merger"><i>Full–fool–foal</i> merger</a> to <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">[ʊɫ~ʊw]</span></li>
<li>Specifically in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Greater_Pittsburgh" title="Greater Pittsburgh">Greater Pittsburgh</a>, <span class="nowrap"><span class="IPA nopopups noexcerpt"><a href="/wiki/Help:IPA/English" title="Help:IPA/English">/<span style="border-bottom:1px dotted"><span title="/aʊ/: 'ou' in 'mouth'">aʊ</span></span>/</a></span></span> is <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">[aʊ~aː]</span>, particularly before <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">/l/</span> and <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">/r/</span>, and in unstressed <a href="/wiki/Function_word" title="Function word">function words</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-25"><a href="#cite_note-25">[24]</a></sup></li></ul>
</div></div></li>
<li><div class="mw-collapsible mw-collapsed" style="box-sizing:border-box;width:100%;font-size:95%;padding:4px;border:none;"><div style="font-size:100%;line-height:1.6;font-weight:bold;background:transparent;text-align:center;;text-align:left"><a href="/wiki/Southern_American_English" title="Southern American English">Southern</a></div><div class="mw-collapsible-content" style="background:transparent;text-align:left;;">
<p>The Southern dialects, including several sub-types, are defined by:
</p>
<ul><li>No (or transitional) <i>cot–caught</i> merger: the <i>cot</i> vowel is <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">[ɑ]</span> and <i>caught</i> vowel is <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">[ɑɒ]</span></li>
<li><span class="nowrap"><span class="IPA nopopups noexcerpt"><a href="/wiki/Help:IPA/English" title="Help:IPA/English">/<span style="border-bottom:1px dotted"><span title="/aɪ/: 'i' in 'tide'">aɪ</span></span>/</a></span></span> is <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">[aː]</span> at least before <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">/b/</span>, <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">/d/</span>, <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">/g/</span>, <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">/v/</span>, or <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">/z/</span>, or word-finally, and potentially elsewhere, the triggering event for the Southern Shift (<span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">[aː]</span> ← <span class="nowrap"><span class="IPA nopopups noexcerpt"><a href="/wiki/Help:IPA/English" title="Help:IPA/English">/<span style="border-bottom:1px dotted"><span title="/aɪ/: 'i' in 'tide'">aɪ</span></span>/</a></span></span> ← <span class="nowrap"><span class="IPA nopopups noexcerpt"><a href="/wiki/Help:IPA/English" title="Help:IPA/English">/<span style="border-bottom:1px dotted"><span title="/eɪ/: 'a' in 'face'">eɪ</span></span>/</a></span></span> ← <span class="nowrap"><span class="IPA nopopups noexcerpt"><a href="/wiki/Help:IPA/English" title="Help:IPA/English">/<span style="border-bottom:1px dotted"><span title="/iː/: 'ee' in 'fleece'">iː</span></span>/</a></span></span>)</li>
<li>"Southern drawl" may break short <a href="/wiki/Front_vowel" title="Front vowel">front vowels</a> into <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Gliding_vowel" title="Gliding vowel">gliding vowels</a>: <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">/æ/</span> → <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">[ɛ(j)ə]</span>; <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">/ɛ/</span> → <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">[ɪ(j)ə]</span>; <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">/ɪ/</span> → <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">[i(j)ə]</span><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Labov_2006_125_26-0"><a href="#cite_note-Labov_2006_125-26">[25]</a></sup></li>
<li><span class="nowrap"><span class="IPA nopopups noexcerpt"><a href="/wiki/Help:IPA/English" title="Help:IPA/English">/<span style="border-bottom:1px dotted"><span title="/aʊ/: 'ou' in 'mouth'">aʊ</span></span>/</a></span></span> is <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">[æo]</span>, the triggering event for the Back Upglide Shift in more advanced sub-types (<span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">[æo]</span> ← <span class="nowrap"><span class="IPA nopopups noexcerpt"><a href="/wiki/Help:IPA/English" title="Help:IPA/English">/<span style="border-bottom:1px dotted"><span title="/aʊ/: 'ou' in 'mouth'">aʊ</span></span>/</a></span></span> ← <span class="nowrap"><span class="IPA nopopups noexcerpt"><a href="/wiki/Help:IPA/English" title="Help:IPA/English">/<span style="border-bottom:1px dotted"><span title="/ɔː/: 'au' in 'fraud'">ɔː</span></span>/</a></span></span> ← <span class="nowrap"><span class="IPA nopopups noexcerpt"><a href="/wiki/Help:IPA/English" title="Help:IPA/English">/<span style="border-bottom:1px dotted"><span title="/ɔɪ/: 'oi' in 'choice'">ɔɪ</span></span>/</a></span></span>)<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-27"><a href="#cite_note-27">[26]</a></sup></li>
<li><span class="nowrap"><span class="IPA nopopups noexcerpt"><a href="/wiki/Help:IPA/English" title="Help:IPA/English">/<span style="border-bottom:1px dotted"><span title="/oʊ/: 'o' in 'code'">oʊ</span></span>/</a></span></span> is <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">[ɜʉ~ɜʊ]</span></li></ul>
</div></div></li>
<li><div class="mw-collapsible mw-collapsed" style="box-sizing:border-box;width:100%;font-size:95%;padding:4px;border:none;"><div style="font-size:100%;line-height:1.6;font-weight:bold;background:transparent;text-align:center;;text-align:left"><a href="/wiki/Mid-Atlantic_American_English" title="Mid-Atlantic American English">Mid-Atlantic</a></div><div class="mw-collapsible-content" style="background:transparent;text-align:left;;">
<p>The Mid-Atlantic ("Delaware Valley") dialect, including Philadelphia and Baltimore sub-types, is defined by:
</p>
<ul><li>No <i>cot–caught</i> merger: the <i>cot</i> vowel is <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">[ä~ɑ]</span> and <i>caught</i> vowel is <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">[ɔə~ʊə]</span>; this severe distinction is the triggering event for the Back Vowel Shift before <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">/r/</span> (<span class="nowrap"><span class="IPA nopopups noexcerpt"><a href="/wiki/Help:IPA/English" title="Help:IPA/English">/<span style="border-bottom:1px dotted"><span title="/ʊər/: 'our' in 'tour'">ʊər</span></span>/</a></span></span> ← <span class="nowrap"><span class="IPA nopopups noexcerpt"><a href="/wiki/Help:IPA/English" title="Help:IPA/English">/<span style="border-bottom:1px dotted"><span title="/ɔːr/: 'ar' in 'war'">ɔːr</span></span>/</a></span></span> ← <span class="nowrap"><span class="IPA nopopups noexcerpt"><a href="/wiki/Help:IPA/English" title="Help:IPA/English">/<span style="border-bottom:1px dotted"><span title="/ɑːr/: 'ar' in 'far'">ɑːr</span></span>/</a></span></span>)<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-28"><a href="#cite_note-28">[27]</a></sup></li>
<li>Unique Mid-Atlantic <span class="nowrap"><span class="IPA nopopups noexcerpt"><a href="/wiki/Help:IPA/English" title="Help:IPA/English">/<span style="border-bottom:1px dotted"><span title="/æ/: 'a' in 'bad'">æ</span></span>/</a></span></span> split system: the <i>bad</i> vowel is <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">[eə]</span> and <i>sad</i> vowel is <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">[æ]</span></li>
<li><span class="nowrap"><span class="IPA nopopups noexcerpt"><a href="/wiki/Help:IPA/English" title="Help:IPA/English">/<span style="border-bottom:1px dotted"><span title="/oʊ/: 'o' in 'code'">oʊ</span></span>/</a></span></span> is <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">[ɜʊ~əʊ]</span></li>
<li><span class="nowrap"><span class="IPA nopopups noexcerpt"><a href="/wiki/Help:IPA/English" title="Help:IPA/English">/<span style="border-bottom:1px dotted"><span title="/aʊ/: 'ou' in 'mouth'">aʊ</span></span>/</a></span></span> is <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">[ɛɔ]</span><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Labov_2006_237_22-1"><a href="#cite_note-Labov_2006_237-22">[21]</a></sup></li>
<li>No <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mary%E2%80%93marry%E2%80%93merry_merger" title="Mary–marry–merry merger">Mary–marry–merry merger</a></li></ul>
</div></div></li>
<li><div class="mw-collapsible mw-collapsed" style="box-sizing:border-box;width:100%;font-size:95%;padding:4px;border:none;"><div style="font-size:100%;line-height:1.6;font-weight:bold;background:transparent;text-align:center;;text-align:left"><a href="/wiki/New_York_City_English" title="New York City English">NYC</a></div><div class="mw-collapsible-content" style="background:transparent;text-align:left;;">
<p>The New York City dialect (with New Orleans English an intermediate sub-type between NYC and Southern) is defined by:
</p>
<ul><li>No <i>cot–caught</i> merger: the <i>cot</i> vowel is <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">[ä~ɑ]</span> and <i>caught</i> vowel is <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">[ɔə~ʊə]</span>; this severe distinction is the triggering event for the Back Vowel Shift before <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">/r/</span> (<span class="nowrap"><span class="IPA nopopups noexcerpt"><a href="/wiki/Help:IPA/English" title="Help:IPA/English">/<span style="border-bottom:1px dotted"><span title="/ʊər/: 'our' in 'tour'">ʊər</span></span>/</a></span></span> ← <span class="nowrap"><span class="IPA nopopups noexcerpt"><a href="/wiki/Help:IPA/English" title="Help:IPA/English">/<span style="border-bottom:1px dotted"><span title="/ɔːr/: 'ar' in 'war'">ɔːr</span></span>/</a></span></span> ← <span class="nowrap"><span class="IPA nopopups noexcerpt"><a href="/wiki/Help:IPA/English" title="Help:IPA/English">/<span style="border-bottom:1px dotted"><span title="/ɑːr/: 'ar' in 'far'">ɑːr</span></span>/</a></span></span>)<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-29"><a href="#cite_note-29">[28]</a></sup></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Rhoticity_in_English" title="Rhoticity in English">Non-rhoticity</a> or variable rhoticity</li>
<li>Unique New York City <span class="nowrap"><span class="IPA nopopups noexcerpt"><a href="/wiki/Help:IPA/English" title="Help:IPA/English">/<span style="border-bottom:1px dotted"><span title="/æ/: 'a' in 'bad'">æ</span></span>/</a></span></span> split system: the <i>bad</i> vowel is <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">[eə]</span> and <i>bat</i> vowel is <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">[æ]</span></li>
<li><span class="nowrap"><span class="IPA nopopups noexcerpt"><a href="/wiki/Help:IPA/English" title="Help:IPA/English">/<span style="border-bottom:1px dotted"><span title="/oʊ/: 'o' in 'code'">oʊ</span></span>/</a></span></span> is <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">[oʊ~ʌʊ]</span> <br/>•No Mary–marry–merry merger</li></ul>
</div></div></li>
<li><div class="mw-collapsible mw-collapsed" style="box-sizing:border-box;width:100%;font-size:95%;padding:4px;border:none;"><div style="font-size:100%;line-height:1.6;font-weight:bold;background:transparent;text-align:center;;text-align:left"><a href="/wiki/Eastern_New_England_English" title="Eastern New England English">ENE</a></div><div class="mw-collapsible-content" style="background:transparent;text-align:left;;">
<p>Eastern New England dialect, including Maine and Boston sub-types (with Rhode Island English an intermediate sub-type between ENE and NYC), is defined by:
</p>
<ul><li><i>Cot–caught</i> merger to <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">[ɒ~ɑ]</span> (lacking only in Rhode Island)</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Rhoticity_in_English" title="Rhoticity in English">Non-rhoticity</a> or variable rhoticity<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Labov_2006_137,_141_30-0"><a href="#cite_note-Labov_2006_137,_141-30">[29]</a></sup></li>
<li><span class="nowrap"><span class="IPA nopopups noexcerpt"><a href="/wiki/Help:IPA/English" title="Help:IPA/English">/<span style="border-bottom:1px dotted"><span title="/aʊ/: 'ou' in 'mouth'">aʊ</span></span>/</a></span></span> is <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">[ɑʊ~äʊ]</span><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Labov_2006_230_31-0"><a href="#cite_note-Labov_2006_230-31">[30]</a></sup><span class="nowrap"><span class="IPA nopopups noexcerpt"><a href="/wiki/Help:IPA/English" title="Help:IPA/English">/<span style="border-bottom:1px dotted"><span title="/oʊ/: 'o' in 'code'">oʊ</span></span>/</a></span></span> is <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">[oʊ~ɔʊ]</span> <br/>•<span class="nowrap"><span class="IPA nopopups noexcerpt"><a href="/wiki/Help:IPA/English" title="Help:IPA/English">/<span style="border-bottom:1px dotted"><span title="/uː/: 'oo' in 'goose'">uː</span></span>/</a></span></span> is <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">[u]</span><br/>• Commonly, beginnings of <span class="nowrap"><span class="IPA nopopups noexcerpt"><a href="/wiki/Help:IPA/English" title="Help:IPA/English">/<span style="border-bottom:1px dotted"><span title="/aɪ/: 'i' in 'tide'">aɪ</span></span>/</a></span></span> and <span class="nowrap"><span class="IPA nopopups noexcerpt"><a href="/wiki/Help:IPA/English" title="Help:IPA/English">/<span style="border-bottom:1px dotted"><span title="/aʊ/: 'ou' in 'mouth'">aʊ</span></span>/</a></span></span> in a <a href="/wiki/Canadian_raising" title="Canadian raising">raised position</a> when before <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Voiceless_consonant" title="Voiceless consonant">voiceless consonants</a>: <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">[əɪ~ʌɪ]</span> and <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">[əʊ~ʌʊ]</span>, respectively</li>
<li>Possibly no <i>Mary–marry–merry</i> merger</li>
<li>No <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Father%E2%80%93bother_merger" title="Father–bother merger"><i>father–bother</i> merger</a> (except in Rhode Island): the <i>father</i> vowel is <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">[a~ä]</span> and <i>bother</i> vowel is <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">[ɒ~ɑ]</span><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-32"><a href="#cite_note-32">[31]</a></sup></li></ul>
</div></div></li>
</ul>
<p>
</p>
</td></tr></tbody></table></div>
<p>Below, eleven major American English accents are defined by their particular combinations of certain characteristics:
</p>
<table class="wikitable sortable">
<tbody><tr>
<th>Accent name</th>
<th>Most populous urban center</th>
<th>Strong <span class="nowrap"><span class="IPA nopopups noexcerpt"><a href="/wiki/Help:IPA/English" title="Help:IPA/English">/<span style="border-bottom:1px dotted"><span title="/aʊ/: 'ou' in 'mouth'">aʊ</span></span>/</a></span></span> fronting</th>
<th>Strong <span class="nowrap"><span class="IPA nopopups noexcerpt"><a href="/wiki/Help:IPA/English" title="Help:IPA/English">/<span style="border-bottom:1px dotted"><span title="/oʊ/: 'o' in 'code'">oʊ</span></span>/</a></span></span> fronting</th>
<th>Strong <span class="nowrap"><span class="IPA nopopups noexcerpt"><a href="/wiki/Help:IPA/English" title="Help:IPA/English">/<span style="border-bottom:1px dotted"><span title="/uː/: 'oo' in 'goose'">uː</span></span>/</a></span></span> fronting</th>
<th>Strong <span class="nowrap"><span class="IPA nopopups noexcerpt"><a href="/wiki/Help:IPA/English" title="Help:IPA/English">/<span style="border-bottom:1px dotted"><span title="/ɑːr/: 'ar' in 'far'">ɑːr</span></span>/</a></span></span> fronting</th>

<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Eastern_New_England">Eastern New England</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=American_English&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: Eastern New England">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Eastern_New_England_English" title="Eastern New England English">Eastern New England English</a></div>
<p><a href="/wiki/Markedness" title="Markedness">Marked</a> New England speech is mostly associated with eastern New England, centering on Boston and Providence, and traditionally includes some notable degree of <a href="/wiki/Rhoticity_in_English" title="Rhoticity in English"><i>r</i>-dropping</a> (or non-rhoticity),<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Labov_2006_137,_141_30-1"><a href="#cite_note-Labov_2006_137,_141-30">[29]</a></sup> as well as the <a href="/wiki/Back_vowel" title="Back vowel">back tongue positioning</a> of the <span class="nowrap"><span class="IPA nopopups noexcerpt"><a href="/wiki/Help:IPA/English" title="Help:IPA/English">/<span style="border-bottom:1px dotted"><span title="/uː/: 'oo' in 'goose'">uː</span></span>/</a></span></span> vowel (to <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">[u]</span>) and the <span class="nowrap"><span class="IPA nopopups noexcerpt"><a href="/wiki/Help:IPA/English" title="Help:IPA/English">/<span style="border-bottom:1px dotted"><span title="/aʊ/: 'ou' in 'mouth'">aʊ</span></span>/</a></span></span> vowel (to <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">[ɑʊ~äʊ]</span>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Labov_2006_230_31-1"><a href="#cite_note-Labov_2006_230-31">[30]</a></sup> In and north of Boston, the <span class="nowrap"><span class="IPA nopopups noexcerpt"><a href="/wiki/Help:IPA/English" title="Help:IPA/English">/<span style="border-bottom:1px dotted"><span title="/ɑːr/: 'ar' in 'far'">ɑːr</span></span>/</a></span></span> sound is famously centralized or even fronted. Boston shows a <a href="/wiki/Cot%E2%80%93caught_merger" title="Cot–caught merger">cot–caught merger</a>, while Providence keeps the same two vowels sharply distinct.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="New_York_City">New York City</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=American_English&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: New York City">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/New_York_City_English" title="New York City English">New York City English</a></div>
<p><a href="/wiki/New_York_City_English" title="New York City English">New York City English</a> prevails in a relatively small but nationally recognizable dialect region in and around <a href="/wiki/New_York_City" title="New York City">New York City</a>, including <a href="/wiki/Long_Island" title="Long Island">Long Island</a> and northeastern <a href="/wiki/New_Jersey" title="New Jersey">New Jersey</a>. The <a href="/wiki/New_York_accent" title="New York accent">New York accent</a> includes some notable degree of <a href="/wiki/Rhoticity_in_English" title="Rhoticity in English">non-rhoticity</a> and a locally unique <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/%C3%86_tensing" title="Æ tensing">short-<i>a</i> vowel pronunciation split</a>. New York City English otherwise broadly follows Northern patterns, except that the <span class="nowrap"><span class="IPA nopopups noexcerpt"><a href="/wiki/Help:IPA/English" title="Help:IPA/English">/<span style="border-bottom:1px dotted"><span title="/aʊ/: 'ou' in 'mouth'">aʊ</span></span>/</a></span></span> vowel is fronted. The <a href="/wiki/Cot%E2%80%93caught_merger" title="Cot–caught merger">cot–caught merger</a> is markedly resisted in the New York metropolitan area, as depicted in popular stereotypes like <i>tawwk</i> and <i>cawwfee</i>, with this <span class="smallcaps" style="font-variant: small-caps; font-size: smaller;">THOUGHT</span> vowel being typically tensed and diphthongal.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="South">South</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=American_English&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: South">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Southern_American_English" title="Southern American English">Southern American English</a></div>
<p>Most <a href="/wiki/Older_Southern_American_English" title="Older Southern American English">older Southern speech</a> along the Gulf and Atlantic coasts was non-rhotic, though, today, almost all Southern dialects are rhotic, and even "hyper-rhotic",<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-34"><a href="#cite_note-34">[33]</a></sup> with a very strongly enunciated, "bunched-tongue" <i>r</i> sound. The modern accent is defined most recognizably by the <span class="nowrap"><span class="IPA nopopups noexcerpt"><a href="/wiki/Help:IPA/English" title="Help:IPA/English">/<span style="border-bottom:1px dotted"><span title="/aɪ/: 'i' in 'tide'">aɪ</span></span>/</a></span></span> vowel losing its <a href="/wiki/Diphthong" title="Diphthong">gliding quality</a> to approach <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">[aː~äː]</span>, the initiation event for the Southern Vowel Shift. This vowel shift involves the "<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Southern_drawl" title="Southern drawl">Southern drawl</a>" that makes short <a href="/wiki/Front_vowel" title="Front vowel">front vowels</a> into distinct-sounding <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Gliding_vowel" title="Gliding vowel">gliding vowels</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Labov_2006_125_26-1"><a href="#cite_note-Labov_2006_125-26">[25]</a></sup> The most advanced sub-varieties exist in the southern Appalachian cities and certain areas of Texas. Non-Southern Americans tend to stereotype Southern accents negatively, associating them with slowness, lack of education, bigotry, and religious or political conservatism,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-35"><a href="#cite_note-35">[34]</a></sup> with labels for the accent such as "hick" or "hillbilly".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-36"><a href="#cite_note-36">[35]</a></sup> Meanwhile, Southerners themselves tend to have mixed judgments of their own accents, some negative but some positively associated with a laid-back, plain, or humble attitude.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-37"><a href="#cite_note-37">[36]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="North">North</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=American_English&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: North">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Northern_American_English" title="Northern American English">Northern American English</a></div>
<p>Since the mid-twentieth century, a distinctive new Northern speech pattern has developed near the Canadian border of the United States, centered on the central and eastern <a href="/wiki/Great_Lakes" title="Great Lakes">Great Lakes</a> region (but only on the American side). Linguists call this region the "<a href="/wiki/Inland_Northern_American_English" title="Inland Northern American English">Inland North</a>", as defined by its <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Northern_Cities_Vowel_Shift" title="Northern Cities Vowel Shift">Northern Cities Vowel Shift</a> (<span class="nowrap"><span class="IPA nopopups noexcerpt"><a href="/wiki/Help:IPA/English" title="Help:IPA/English">/<span style="border-bottom:1px dotted"><span title="/æ/: 'a' in 'bad'">æ</span></span>/</a></span></span> raising, <span class="nowrap"><span class="IPA nopopups noexcerpt"><a href="/wiki/Help:IPA/English" title="Help:IPA/English">/<span style="border-bottom:1px dotted"><span title="/ɑː/: 'a' in 'father'">ɑː</span></span>/</a></span></span> fronting, and other vowel changes), occurring in the same region whose "standard Midwestern" speech was the basis for <a href="/wiki/General_American" title="General American">General American</a> in the mid-twentieth century, though prior to the full Northern Cities Vowel Shift. The Inland Northern accent was lampooned on the television show <i><a href="/wiki/Saturday_Night_Live" title="Saturday Night Live">Saturday Night Live</a>'</i>s "<a href="/wiki/Bill_Swerski%27s_Superfans" title="Bill Swerski's Superfans">Bill Swerski's Superfans</a>" segments, though the accent's shift may be reversing in certain communities.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-lansing_38-0"><a href="#cite_note-lansing-38">[37]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-syracuse_39-0"><a href="#cite_note-syracuse-39">[38]</a></sup> Many people view the "<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/North_Central_American_English" title="North Central American English">North Central</a>" or "<a href="/wiki/Upper_Midwest" title="Upper Midwest">Upper Midwestern</a>" accent, another Northern accent, from the stereotypical lens of the movie <i><a href="/wiki/Fargo_(film)" title="Fargo (film)">Fargo</a></i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-40"><a href="#cite_note-40">[39]</a></sup> The North Central accent is characterized by a more common <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Cot-caught_merger" title="Cot-caught merger">cot-caught merger</a> and influences from the German and Scandinavian settlers of the region.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Midland">Midland</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=American_English&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Midland">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Midland_American_English" title="Midland American English">Midland American English</a></div>
<p>Between the traditional American dialect areas of the "North" and "South" is what linguists have long called the "Midland" encompassing states situated in the lower Midwest, beginning west of the <a href="/wiki/Appalachian_Mountains" title="Appalachian Mountains">Appalachian Mountains</a>. The vocabulary of its older speakers was divided into two discrete subdivisions, the "North Midland" that begins north of the <a href="/wiki/Ohio_River" title="Ohio River">Ohio River</a> valley area, and the "South Midland" speech, which to the average American ear has a slight trace of the "<a href="/wiki/Southern_American_English" title="Southern American English">Southern accent</a>" (especially due to some degree of <span class="nowrap"><span class="IPA nopopups noexcerpt"><a href="/wiki/Help:IPA/English" title="Help:IPA/English">/<span style="border-bottom:1px dotted"><span title="/aɪ/: 'i' in 'tide'">aɪ</span></span>/</a></span></span> glide weakening). Modern Midland speech is transitional between a presence and absence of the <a href="/wiki/Cot%E2%80%93caught_merger" title="Cot–caught merger">cot–caught merger</a>. Historically, Pennsylvania was a home of the Midland dialect; however, this state of early English-speaking settlers has now largely split off into new dialect regions, with distinct <a href="/wiki/Philadelphia_English" title="Philadelphia English">Philadelphia</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Pittsburgh_dialect" title="Pittsburgh dialect">Pittsburgh dialects</a> documented since the middle of the twentieth century.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="West">West</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=American_English&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: West">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Western_American_English" title="Western American English">Western American English</a></div>
<p>A generalized Midland speech continues westward until becoming a somewhat internally diverse <a href="/wiki/Western_American_English" title="Western American English">Western American English</a> that unites the entire western half of the country, mostly unified by a firm <a href="/wiki/Cot%E2%80%93caught_merger" title="Cot–caught merger">cot–caught merger</a> and a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Conservative_(linguistics)" title="Conservative (linguistics)">conservatively</a> backed pronunciation of the long <i>oh</i> sound in <i>goat, toe, show</i>, etc., but a fronted pronunciation of the long <i>oo</i> sound in <i>goose, lose, tune,</i> etc. Western speech itself contains such advanced sub-types as <a href="/wiki/Pacific_Northwest_English" title="Pacific Northwest English">Pacific Northwest English</a> and <a href="/wiki/California_English" title="California English">California English</a>, with the native-speaker <a href="/wiki/Chicano_English" title="Chicano English">English of Mexican Americans</a> also being a sub-type primarily of the Western dialect. The island state of <a href="/wiki/Hawaii" title="Hawaii">Hawaii</a>, though primarily English-speaking, is also home to a <a href="/wiki/Creole_language" title="Creole language">creole language</a> known commonly as <a href="/wiki/Hawaiian_Pidgin" title="Hawaiian Pidgin">Hawaiian Pidgin</a>, and some Hawaii residents speak English with a Pidgin-influenced accent.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Other_varieties">Other varieties</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=American_English&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Other varieties">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Although no longer region-specific,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-41"><a href="#cite_note-41">[40]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/African-American_Vernacular_English" title="African-American Vernacular English">African-American Vernacular English</a>, which remains the native variety of most working- and middle-class <a href="/wiki/African_Americans" title="African Americans">African Americans</a>, has a close relationship to Southern dialects and has greatly influenced everyday speech of many Americans, including <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hip_hop_culture" title="Hip hop culture">hip hop culture</a>. <a href="/wiki/Hispanic_and_Latino_Americans" title="Hispanic and Latino Americans">Hispanic and Latino Americans</a> have also developed native-speaker varieties of English. The best-studied Latino Englishes are <a href="/wiki/Chicano_English" title="Chicano English">Chicano English</a>, spoken in the West and Midwest, and <a href="/wiki/New_York_Latino_English" title="New York Latino English">New York Latino English</a>, spoken in the <a href="/wiki/New_York_metropolitan_area" title="New York metropolitan area">New York metropolitan area</a>. Additionally, ethnic varieties such as <a href="/wiki/Yeshivish" title="Yeshivish">Yeshiva English</a> and "<a href="/wiki/Yinglish" title="Yinglish">Yinglish</a>" are spoken by some <a href="/wiki/American_Jews" title="American Jews">American Jews</a>, <a href="/wiki/Cajun_English" title="Cajun English">Cajun Vernacular English</a> by some <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Cajun" title="Cajun">Cajuns</a> in southern Louisiana, <a href="/wiki/Pennsylvania_Dutch_English" title="Pennsylvania Dutch English">Pennsylvania Dutch English</a> by some <a href="/wiki/Pennsylvania_Dutch" title="Pennsylvania Dutch">Pennsylvania Dutch</a> in Pennsylvania and the Midwest, and <a href="/wiki/American_Indian_English" title="American Indian English">American Indian Englishes</a> have been documented among diverse Indian tribes.
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Phonology">Phonology</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=American_English&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: Phonology">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/General_American" title="General American">General American</a> and <a href="/wiki/North_American_English_regional_phonology" title="North American English regional phonology">North American English regional phonology</a></div>
<p>Compared with <a href="/wiki/English_language_in_England" title="English language in England">English as spoken in England</a>, North American English<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-42"><a href="#cite_note-42">[41]</a></sup> is more homogeneous, and any North American accent that exhibits a majority of the most common phonological features is known as "<a href="/wiki/General_American" title="General American">General American</a>." This section mostly refers to such widespread or mainstream pronunciation features that characterize American English.
</p><p>Studies on historical usage of English in both the United States and the United Kingdom suggest that spoken American English did not simply deviate away from period British English, but retained certain now-archaic features contemporary <a href="/wiki/British_English" title="British English">British English</a> has since lost.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-43"><a href="#cite_note-43">[42]</a></sup> One of these is the rhoticity common in most American accents, because in the 17th century, when English was brought to the Americas, most English in England was also rhotic. The preservation of rhoticity has been further supported by the influences of <a href="/wiki/Hiberno-English" title="Hiberno-English">Hiberno-English</a>, <a href="/wiki/West_Country_English" title="West Country English">West Country English</a> and <a href="/wiki/Scottish_English" title="Scottish English">Scottish English</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-44"><a href="#cite_note-44">[43]</a></sup> In most varieties of North American English, the sound corresponding to the letter ⟨r⟩ is a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Postalveolar_approximant" title="Postalveolar approximant">postalveolar approximant</a> <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">[ɹ̠]</span> or <a href="/wiki/Retroflex_approximant" title="Retroflex approximant">retroflex approximant</a> <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">[ɻ]</span> rather than a trill or tap (as often heard, for example, in the English accents of Scotland or India). A unique "bunched tongue" variant of the approximant <i>r</i> sound is also associated with the United States, and seems particularly noticeable in the Midwest and South.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-45"><a href="#cite_note-45">[44]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:252px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Non-RhoticityUSA.png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="557" data-file-width="392" height="355" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2d/Non-RhoticityUSA.png/250px-Non-RhoticityUSA.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2d/Non-RhoticityUSA.png/375px-Non-RhoticityUSA.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/2/2d/Non-RhoticityUSA.png 2x" width="250"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Non-RhoticityUSA.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The red dots show every U.S. metropolitan area where over 50% non-rhotic speech has been documented among some of that area's local white speakers. Non-rhotic speech may be heard from <a href="/wiki/African-American_English" title="African-American English">black speakers</a> throughout the whole country.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-46"><a href="#cite_note-46">[45]</a></sup></div></div></div>
<p>Traditionally, the "East Coast" comprises three or four major linguistically distinct regions, each of which possesses English varieties both distinct from each other as well as quite internally diverse: <a href="/wiki/New_England" title="New England">New England</a>, the <a href="/wiki/New_York_metropolitan_area" title="New York metropolitan area">New York metropolitan area</a>, the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mid-Atlantic_states" title="Mid-Atlantic states">Mid-Atlantic states</a> (centering on Philadelphia and Baltimore), and the <a href="/wiki/Southern_United_States" title="Southern United States">Southern United States</a>. The only traditionally <a href="/wiki/Rhoticity_in_English" title="Rhoticity in English"><i>r</i>-dropping</a> (or non-rhotic) regional accents of American English are all spoken along the Atlantic Coast and parts of the Gulf Coast (particularly still in Louisiana), because these areas were in close historical contact with <a href="/wiki/England" title="England">England</a> and imitated prestigious varieties of <i>r</i>-dropping London (a feature now widespread throughout most of England) at a time when they were undergoing changes.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-47"><a href="#cite_note-47">[46]</a></sup> Today, non-rhoticity is confined in the United States to the accents of <a href="/wiki/New_England_English" title="New England English">eastern New England</a>, <a href="/wiki/New_York_City_English" title="New York City English">New York City</a>, older speakers of the <a href="/wiki/Older_Southern_American_English" title="Older Southern American English">former plantation South</a>, and <a href="/wiki/African-American_Vernacular_English" title="African-American Vernacular English">African-American Vernacular English</a> (though the vowel-consonant cluster found in "bird", "work", "hurt", "learn", etc. usually retains its <i>r</i> pronunciation, even in these non-rhotic accents). Other than these few varieties, American accents are <a href="/wiki/Rhoticity_in_English" title="Rhoticity in English">rhotic</a>, pronouncing every instance of the ⟨r⟩ sound.
</p><p>Many British accents have evolved in other ways compared to which <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/General_American_English" title="General American English">General American English</a> has remained relatively more <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Conservative_(linguistics)" title="Conservative (linguistics)">conservative</a>, for example, regarding the typical southern British features of a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Trap%E2%80%93bath_split" title="Trap–bath split">trap–bath split</a>, fronting of <span class="nowrap"><span class="IPA nopopups noexcerpt"><a href="/wiki/Help:IPA/English" title="Help:IPA/English">/<span style="border-bottom:1px dotted"><span title="/oʊ/: 'o' in 'code'">oʊ</span></span>/</a></span></span>, and <a href="/wiki/H-dropping" title="H-dropping">H-dropping</a>, none of which typical American accents show. The innovation of <a href="/wiki/T-glottalization" title="T-glottalization">/t/ glottaling</a>, which does occur before a consonant (including a syllabic <a href="/wiki/Coronal_consonant" title="Coronal consonant">coronal</a> <a href="/wiki/Nasal_consonant" title="Nasal consonant">nasal consonant</a>, like in the words <i>button</i> or <i>satin</i>) and word-finally in General American, additionally occurs variably between vowels in British English. On the other hand, General American is more innovative than the dialects of England, or English elsewhere in the world, in a number of its own ways:
</p>
<ul><li>The <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Phonological_history_of_the_low_back_vowels#Father–bother_merger" title="Phonological history of the low back vowels">merger of <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">/ɑ/</span> and <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">/ɒ/</span></a>, making <i>father</i> and <i>bother</i> rhyme. This change, known as the <i>father–bother</i> merger is in a transitional or completed stage nearly universally in North American English. Exceptions are in northeastern <a href="/wiki/New_England_English" title="New England English">New England English</a>, such as the <a href="/wiki/Boston_accent" title="Boston accent">Boston accent</a>, <a href="/wiki/New_York_accent" title="New York accent">New York accent</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Mid-Atlantic_American_English" title="Mid-Atlantic American English">Mid-Atlantic States accents</a>, and many <a href="/wiki/Southern_American_English" title="Southern American English">Southern accents</a>, prominently including <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Yat_dialect" title="Yat dialect">New Orleans accents</a>.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="margin-left:0.1em; white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Please_clarify" title="Wikipedia:Please clarify"><span title='Verification needed for "many" Southern dialects. As New York City English had an influence on the accent of New Orleans, this may actually be a case of diffusion of a New York City feature into New Orleans. (May 2017)'>clarification needed</span></a></i>]</sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Wells_48-0"><a href="#cite_note-Wells-48">[47]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-49"><a href="#cite_note-49">[48]</a></sup></li>
<li>About half of all Americans merge of the vowels <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">/ɑ/</span> and <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">/ɔ/</span>. This is the so-called <a href="/wiki/Cot%E2%80%93caught_merger" title="Cot–caught merger">cot–caught merger</a>, where words like <i>cot</i> and <i>caught</i> are <a href="/wiki/Homophone" title="Homophone">homophones</a>. This change has occurred most firmly in eastern New England (Boston area), <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Pittsburgh_English" title="Pittsburgh English">Greater Pittsburgh</a>, and the whole western half of the country.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-50"><a href="#cite_note-50">[49]</a></sup></li>
<li>For speakers who do not merge <i>caught</i> and <i>cot</i>, the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Lot%E2%80%93cloth_split" title="Lot–cloth split">lot–cloth split</a> has taken hold. This change took place prior to the unrounding of the <i>cot</i>.  It is the result of the lengthening and raising of the <i>cot</i> vowel, merging with the <i>caught</i> vowel in many cases before <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Voiceless_fricative" title="Voiceless fricative">voiceless fricatives</a> (as in <i>cloth, off</i>), which is also found in some varieties of British English, as well as before <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">/ŋ/</span> (as in <i>strong, long),</i> usually in <i>gone,</i> often in <i>on,</i> and irregularly before <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">/ɡ/</span> <i>(log, hog, dog, fog</i>).</li>
<li>The <i>strut</i> vowel, rather than the <i>lot</i> or <i>thought</i> vowel, is used in the function words <i>was, of, from, what, everybody, nobody, somebody, anybody</i>, and, for some speakers, <i>because</i> and <i>want</i>, when stressed.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-51"><a href="#cite_note-51">[50]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-52"><a href="#cite_note-52">[51]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-53"><a href="#cite_note-53">[52]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-54"><a href="#cite_note-54">[53]</a></sup></li>
<li><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/English-language_vowel_changes_before_historic_r" title="English-language vowel changes before historic r">Vowel mergers</a> before intervocalic <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">/ɹ/</span>: The <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mary%E2%80%93marry%E2%80%93merry_merger" title="Mary–marry–merry merger">Mary–marry–merry</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mirror%E2%80%93nearer_merger" title="Mirror–nearer merger">serious–Sirius</a>, and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/English-language_vowel_changes_before_historic_r#Hurry–furry_merger" title="English-language vowel changes before historic r">hurry–furry</a> mergers are found in most American English dialects.  However, exceptions exist primarily along the east coast.
<ul><li>Americans vary slightly in their pronunciations of <a href="/wiki/R-colored_vowel#Rhotacization" title="R-colored vowel">R-colored vowels</a>—such as those in <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">/ɛəɹ/</span> and <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">/ɪəɹ/</span>—sometimes monophthongizing towards <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">[ɛɹ]</span> and <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">[ɪɹ]</span> or <a href="/wiki/Tenseness" title="Tenseness">tensing</a> towards <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">[eɪɹ]</span> and <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">[i(ə)ɹ]</span> respectively, causing pronunciations like <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">[peɪɹ]</span> for <i>pair</i>/<i>pear</i> and <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">[piəɹ]</span> for <i>peer</i>/<i>pier</i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-55"><a href="#cite_note-55">[54]</a></sup> Also, <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">/jʊər/</span> is often reduced to <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">[jɚ]</span>, so that <i>cure</i>, <i>pure</i>, and <i>mature</i> may all end with the sound <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">[ɚ]</span>, thus rhyming with <i>blur</i> and <i>sir</i>. The word <i>sure</i> is also part of this rhyming set as it is commonly pronounced <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">[ʃɚ]</span>.</li></ul></li>
<li><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/English_consonant-cluster_reductions#''Yod''-dropping" title="English consonant-cluster reductions">Dropping of <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">/j/</span></a> is much more extensive than in most of England. In most North American accents, <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">/j/</span> is dropped after all <a href="/wiki/Alveolar_consonant" title="Alveolar consonant">alveolar</a> and interdental consonants (i.e. everywhere except after /p/, /b/, /f/, /h/, /k/, and /m/) so that <i>new, duke, Tuesday, presume</i> are pronounced <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">[nu]</span>, <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">[duk]</span>, <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">[ˈtuzdeɪ]</span>, <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">[pɹɪˈzum]</span>.</li>
<li><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki//%C3%A6/_tensing" title="/æ/ tensing">/æ/ tensing</a> in environments that vary widely from accent to accent. With most American speakers, for whom the phoneme <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">/æ/</span> operates under a somewhat continuous system, <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">/æ/</span> has both a tense and a lax <a href="/wiki/Allophone" title="Allophone">allophone</a> (with a kind of "continuum" of possible sounds between those two extremes, rather than a definitive split). In these accents, <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">/æ/</span> is overall realized before <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Nasal_stop" title="Nasal stop">nasal stops</a> as more tense (approximately <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">[eə̯]</span>), while other environments are more lax (approximately the standard <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">[æ]</span>); for example, note the vowel sound in <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">[mæs]</span> for <i>mass</i>, but <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">[meə̯n]</span> for <i>man</i>). In some American accents, though, specifically those from <a href="/wiki/Baltimore" title="Baltimore">Baltimore</a>, <a href="/wiki/Philadelphia" title="Philadelphia">Philadelphia</a>, and <a href="/wiki/New_York_City" title="New York City">New York City</a>, <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">[æ]</span> and <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">[eə̯]</span> are entirely separate (or "split") phonemes, for example, in <i>planet</i> <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">[pɫænɪ̈t̚]</span> vs. <i>plan it</i> <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">[pɫeənɪ̈t̚]</span>. This is often called the Mid-Atlantic <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki//%C3%A6/#Phonemic_tensing" title="/æ/">split-<i>a</i></a> system. Note that these vowels move in the opposite direction in the mouth compared to the backed British "<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Broad_A" title="Broad A">broad A</a>"; this phenomenon has been noted as related to the increasingly rare phenomenon of older speakers of the eastern New England (Boston) area for whom <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">/æ/</span> changes to <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">/ɑ/</span> before <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">/f/, /s/, /θ/, /ð/, /z/, /v/</span> alone or when preceded by a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Homorganic" title="Homorganic">homorganic</a> nasal.</li></ul>

<i>arable, arid, baron, barrel, barren, carry, carrot, chariot, charity, clarity, Gary, Harry, Larry, marionette, maritime, marry, marriage, paragon, parent, parish, parody, parrot,</i> etc.; this feature is determined by the presence or absence of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mary-marry-merry_merger" title="Mary-marry-merry merger">Mary-marry-merry merger</a></div></div>

<td><div class="mw-collapsible mw-collapsed" style="box-sizing:border-box;width:100%;font-size:95%;padding:4px;border:none;"><div style="font-size:100%;line-height:1.6;font-weight:bold;background:transparent;text-align:center;;"></div><div class="mw-collapsible-content" style="background:transparent;text-align:left;;">
<i>Alexander, answer, ant, band, can </i>(the noun)<i>, can't, clam, dance, ham, hamburger, hand, handy, man, manly, pants, plan, planning, ranch, sand, slant, tan, understand</i>, etc.; in Philadelphia, <i>began, ran,</i> and <i>swam</i> alone remain lax</div></div>

<td colspan="2"><span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">[eə]</span>
