<tr>
<td><span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)"><span class="nowrap">/b/,</span> <span class="nowrap">/d/,</span> <span class="nowrap">/dʒ/,</span> <span class="nowrap">/ʃ/,</span> <span class="nowrap">/v/,</span> <span class="nowrap">/z/,</span> <span class="nowrap">/ʒ/</span></span>
</td>
<td>Closed
</td>
<td><div class="mw-collapsible mw-collapsed" style="box-sizing:border-box;width:100%;font-size:95%;padding:4px;border:none;"><div style="font-size:100%;line-height:1.6;font-weight:bold;background:transparent;text-align:center;;"></div><div class="mw-collapsible-content" style="background:transparent;text-align:left;;">
<i>absolve, abstain, add, ash, as, bad, badge, bash, cab, cash, clad, crag, dad, drab, fad, flash, glad, grab, had, halve </i>(varies by speaker)<i>, jazz </i>(varies by speaker)<i>, kashmir, mad, magnet, pad, plaid, rag, raspberry, rash, sad, sag, smash, splash, tab, tadpole, trash,</i> etc. In NYC, this environment, particularly, <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">/v/</span> and <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">/z/</span>, has a lot of variance and many exceptions to the rules. In Philadelphia, <i>bad, mad,</i> and <i>glad</i> alone in this set become tense. Similarly, in New York City, the <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">/dʒ/</span> set is often tense even in open syllables (<i>magic</i>, <i>imagine</i>, etc.)</div></div>
</td>

<td colspan="2">All other consonants
</td>
<td><div class="mw-collapsible mw-collapsed" style="box-sizing:border-box;width:100%;font-size:95%;padding:4px;border:none;"><div style="font-size:100%;line-height:1.6;font-weight:bold;background:transparent;text-align:center;;"></div><div class="mw-collapsible-content" style="background:transparent;text-align:left;;">
<i>act, agony, allergy, apple, aspirin, athlete, avid, back, bat, brat, café, cafeteria, cap, cashew, cat, Catholic, chap, clap, classy, diagonal, fashion, fat, flap, flat, gap, gnat, latch, magazine, mallet, map, mastiff, match, maverick, Max, pack, pal, passive, passion, pat, patch, pattern, rabid, racket, rally, rap, rat, sack, sat, Saturn, savvy, scratch, shack, slack, slap, tackle, talent, trap, travel, wrap</i>, etc.</div></div>
</td>
<td colspan="2"><span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">[æ]</span>
</td></tr>
<tr>
<td colspan="14"><div class="mw-collapsible mw-collapsed" style="box-sizing:border-box;width:100%;font-size:95%;padding:4px;border:none;"><div style="font-size:100%;line-height:1.6;font-weight:bold;background:transparent;text-align:center;;text-align: left;">Footnotes</div><div class="mw-collapsible-content" style="background:transparent;text-align:left;;">
<ol><li>Nearly all American English speakers pronounce <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">/æŋ/</span> somewhere between <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">[æŋ]</span> and <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">[eɪŋ]</span>, though <a href="/wiki/Western_American_English" title="Western American English">Western speakers</a> specifically favor <small></small><span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)"><a href="/wiki/Help:IPA" title="Help:IPA">[eɪŋ]</a></span>.</li>
<li>The Great Lakes dialect traditionally tenses <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">/æ/</span> in all cases, but reversals of that tensing before non-nasal consonants (while often maintaining some of the other vowel shifts of the region) has been observed recently where it has been studied, in <a href="/wiki/Lansing,_Michigan" title="Lansing, Michigan">Lansing</a> and <a href="/wiki/Syracuse,_New_York" title="Syracuse, New York">Syracuse</a>.</li>
<li>The NYC, Philadelphia, and Baltimore dialects' rule of tensing <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">/æ/</span> in certain closed-syllable environments also applies to words <a href="/wiki/Inflection" title="Inflection">inflectionally</a> derived from those closed-syllable <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">/æ/</span> environments that now have an open-syllable <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">/æ/</span>. For example, in addition to <i>pass</i> being tense (according to the general rule), so are its open-syllable derivatives <i>passing</i> and <i>passer-by</i>, but not <i>passive</i>.</li></ol></div></div>
</td></tr></tbody></table>
<ul><li><a href="/wiki/Flapping" title="Flapping">Flapping</a> of intervocalic <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">/t/</span> and <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">/d/</span> to <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Alveolar_tap" title="Alveolar tap">alveolar tap</a> <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">[ɾ]</span> before unstressed vowels (as in "bu<i>tt</i>er" <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">[ˈbʌɾəɹ]</span>, "par<i>t</i>y" <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">[ˈpɑɹɾi]</span>) and syllabic <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">/l/</span> ("bo<i>tt</i>le" <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">[ˈbɑɾəɫ]</span>), as well as at the end of a word or morpheme before any vowel ("wha<i>t</i> else" <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">[wʌˈɾɛɫs]</span>, "wha<i>t</i>ever" <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">[wʌˈɾɛvəɹ]</span>). Thus, for most speakers, pairs such as <i>ladder/latter, metal/medal,</i> and <i>coating/coding</i> are pronounced the same, except with the stressed <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">/aɪ/</span> (see below).</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Canadian_raising" title="Canadian raising">Canadian raising</a> of <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">/aɪ/</span>: many speakers split the sound <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">/aɪ/</span> based on its presence before either a voiceless or voiced consonant, so that in <i>writer</i> it is pronounced <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">[ʌɪ]</span> but in <i>rider</i> it is pronounced <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">[äɪ]</span> (because <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">[t]</span> is a voiceless consonant while <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">[d]</span> is voiced). This is a form of <a href="/wiki/Canadian_raising" title="Canadian raising">Canadian raising</a> but, unlike more extreme forms of that process, does not affect <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">/aʊ/</span>. In many areas and idiolects, a distinction between what elsewhere become homophones through this process is maintained by vowel lengthening in the vowel preceding the formerly voiced consonant, <i>e.g.</i>, <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">[ˈɹʌɪɾɚ]</span> for "writer" as opposed to <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">[ˈɹäɪɾɚ]</span> for "rider".
<ul><li>Many speakers in the <a href="/wiki/Inland_Northern_American_English" title="Inland Northern American English">Inland North</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/North_Central_American_English" title="North Central American English">North Central American English</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Philadelphia_English" title="Philadelphia English">Philadelphia</a> dialect areas raise <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">/aɪ/</span> before voiced consonants in certain words as well, particularly <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">[d]</span>, <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">[g]</span> and <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">[n]</span>. Hence, words like <i>tiny</i>, <i>spider</i>, <i>cider</i>, <i>tiger</i>, <i>dinosaur</i>, <i>cyber-</i>, <i>beside</i>, <i>idle</i> (but sometimes not <i>idol</i>), and <i>fire</i> may contain a raised nucleus. The use of <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">[ʌɪ]</span> rather than <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">[aɪ]</span> in such words is unpredictable from phonetic environment alone, though it may have to do with their acoustic similarity to other words that <i>do</i> contain <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">[ʌɪ]</span> before a voiceless consonant, per the traditional Canadian-raising system. Hence, some researchers have argued that there has been a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Phonemic_split" title="Phonemic split">phonemic split</a> in these dialects; the distribution of the two sounds is becoming more unpredictable among younger speakers.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-58"><a href="#cite_note-58">[57]</a></sup></li></ul></li>
<li>L-velarization: England's typical distinction between a "clear L" (i.e. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/L_(IPA)" title="L (IPA)"><span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">[l]</span></a>) and a "dark L" (i.e. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Velarized_alveolar_lateral_approximant" title="Velarized alveolar lateral approximant"><span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">[ɫ]</span></a> or sometimes even <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Velar_lateral_approximant" title="Velar lateral approximant"><span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">[ʟ]</span></a>) is much less noticeable in nearly all dialects of American English; it is often altogether absent.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-59"><a href="#cite_note-59">[58]</a></sup> Instead, most U.S. speakers pronounce all "L" sounds with a tendency to be "dark", meaning with some degree of <a href="/wiki/Velarization" title="Velarization">velarization</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-60"><a href="#cite_note-60">[59]</a></sup> The only notable exceptions to this are in some Spanish-influenced U.S. English varieties (such as <a href="/wiki/New_York_Latino_English" title="New York Latino English">East Coast Latino English</a>, which typically shows a clear "L" in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Syllable_onset" title="Syllable onset">syllable onsets</a>); in <a href="/wiki/New_York_City_English" title="New York City English">New York City English</a>, where the <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">/l/</span> is clear in prevocalic positions;<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-61"><a href="#cite_note-61">[60]</a></sup> and in <a href="/wiki/Older_Southern_American_English" title="Older Southern American English">older, moribund Southern speech</a> of the U.S., where "L" is clear in an <a href="/wiki/Intervocalic_consonant" title="Intervocalic consonant">intervocalic</a> environment between <a href="/wiki/Front_vowel" title="Front vowel">front vowels</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-62"><a href="#cite_note-62">[61]</a></sup></li>
<li>Both intervocalic <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">/nt/</span> and <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">/n/</span> may commonly be realized as <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">[ɾ̃]</span> or simply <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">[n]</span>, making <i>winter</i> and <i>winner</i> homophones in fast or non-careful speech.</li>
<li>The vowel <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">/ɪ/</span> in unstressed syllables generally merges with <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">/ə/</span> (<a href="/wiki/Phonological_history_of_English_high_front_vowels#Weak-vowel_merger" title="Phonological history of English high front vowels">weak-vowel merger</a>), so <i>effect</i> is pronounced like <i>affect</i>.</li></ul>
<p>Some mergers found in most varieties of both American and British English include:
</p>
<ul><li><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Horse%E2%80%93hoarse_merger" title="Horse–hoarse merger">Horse–hoarse merger</a>, making the vowels <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">/ɔ/</span> and <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">/o/</span> before 'r' homophones, with homophonous pairs like <i>horse/hoarse, corps/core, for/four, morning/mourning, war/wore,</i> etc. <a href="/wiki/Homophone" title="Homophone">homophones</a>.</li>
<li><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Wine%E2%80%93whine_merger" title="Wine–whine merger">Wine–whine merger</a>, making pairs like <i>wine/whine, wet/whet, Wales/whales, wear/where,</i> etc. <a href="/wiki/Homophone" title="Homophone">homophones</a>, in most cases eliminating <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">/ʍ/</span>, the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Voiceless_labiovelar_fricative" title="Voiceless labiovelar fricative">voiceless labiovelar fricative</a>. Many older varieties of southern and western American English still keep these distinct, but the merger appears to be spreading.</li></ul>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Vocabulary">Vocabulary</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=American_English&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: Vocabulary">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/American_English_vocabulary" title="American English vocabulary">American English vocabulary</a></div>
<p>The process of coining new lexical items started as soon as English-speaking British-American colonists began borrowing names for unfamiliar flora, fauna, and topography from the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Native_American_languages" title="Native American languages">Native American languages</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Skeat_63-0"><a href="#cite_note-Skeat-63">[62]</a></sup> Examples of such names are <i><a href="/wiki/Virginia_opossum" title="Virginia opossum">opossum</a>, <a href="/wiki/Raccoon" title="Raccoon">raccoon</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Squash_(fruit)" title="Squash (fruit)">squash</a></i>, <i><a href="/wiki/Moose" title="Moose">moose</a></i> (from <a href="/wiki/Algonquian_languages" title="Algonquian languages">Algonquian</a>),<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Skeat_63-1"><a href="#cite_note-Skeat-63">[62]</a></sup> <i><a href="/wiki/Wigwam" title="Wigwam">wigwam</a></i>, and <i><a href="/wiki/Moccasin" title="Moccasin">moccasin</a></i>. The languages of the other colonizing nations also added to the American vocabulary; for instance, <i><a href="/wiki/Cookie" title="Cookie">cookie</a></i>, from <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Dutch_Language" title="Dutch Language">Dutch</a>; <i><a href="/wiki/Kindergarten" title="Kindergarten">kindergarten</a></i> from <a href="/wiki/German_language" title="German language">German</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-64"><a href="#cite_note-64">[63]</a></sup> <i><a href="/wiki/Levee" title="Levee">levee</a></i> from <a href="/wiki/French_language" title="French language">French</a>; and <i><a href="/wiki/Rodeo" title="Rodeo">rodeo</a></i> from <a href="/wiki/Spanish_language" title="Spanish language">Spanish</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-65"><a href="#cite_note-65">[64]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-66"><a href="#cite_note-66">[65]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-67"><a href="#cite_note-67">[66]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-68"><a href="#cite_note-68">[67]</a></sup> Landscape features are often loanwords from French or Spanish, and the word <i><a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/corn" title="wikt:corn">corn</a></i>, used in England to refer to wheat (or any cereal), came to denote the <a href="/wiki/Maize" title="Maize">maize</a> plant, the most important crop in the U.S.
</p><p>Most <a href="/wiki/Mexican_Spanish" title="Mexican Spanish">Mexican Spanish</a> contributions came after the <a href="/wiki/War_of_1812" title="War of 1812">War of 1812</a>, with the opening of the West, like <i><a href="/wiki/Ranch" title="Ranch">ranch</a></i> (now a common <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ranch_house" title="Ranch house">house style</a>). New forms of dwelling created new terms <i>(<a href="/wiki/Land_lot" title="Land lot">lot</a>, waterfront)</i> and types of homes like <i><a href="/wiki/Log_cabin" title="Log cabin">log cabin</a>, <a href="/wiki/Adobe" title="Adobe">adobe</a></i> in the 18th century; <i><a href="/wiki/Apartment" title="Apartment">apartment</a>, <a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/shanty" title="wikt:shanty">shanty</a></i> in the 19th century; <i>project, <a href="/wiki/Condominium" title="Condominium">condominium</a>, <a href="/wiki/Townhouse" title="Townhouse">townhouse</a>, <a href="/wiki/Mobile_home" title="Mobile home">mobile home</a></i> in the 20th century; and parts thereof <i>(<a href="/wiki/Driveway" title="Driveway">driveway</a>, breezeway, <a href="/wiki/Backyard" title="Backyard">backyard</a>)</i>.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (April 2010)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> Industry and material innovations from the 19th century onwards provide distinctive new words, phrases, and idioms through <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Railroad" title="Railroad">railroading</a> (see further at <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Rail_terminology" title="Rail terminology">rail terminology</a>) and <a href="/wiki/Transport" title="Transport">transportation</a> terminology, ranging from types of roads (<i>dirt roads</i>, <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Freeway" title="Freeway">freeways</a></i>) to infrastructure <i>(<a href="/wiki/Parking_lot" title="Parking lot">parking lot</a>, <a href="/wiki/Overpass" title="Overpass">overpass</a>, <a href="/wiki/Rest_area" title="Rest area">rest area</a>),</i> to automotive terminology often now standard in English internationally.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-69"><a href="#cite_note-69">[68]</a></sup> Already existing English words—such as <i><a href="/wiki/General_store" title="General store">store</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Retailing" title="Retailing">shop</a>, <a href="/wiki/Lumber" title="Lumber">lumber</a></i>—underwent shifts in meaning; others remained in the U.S. while changing in Britain. From the world of business and finance came new terms (<i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Merger" title="Merger">merger</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Downsize" title="Downsize">downsize</a>, <a href="/wiki/Net_income" title="Net income">bottom line</a></i>), from sports and gambling terminology came, specific jargon aside, common everyday American idioms, including <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/English_language_idioms_derived_from_baseball" title="English language idioms derived from baseball">many idioms related to baseball</a>. The names of some American inventions remained largely confined to North America (<i><a href="/wiki/Elevator" title="Elevator">elevator</a>, <a href="/wiki/Gasoline" title="Gasoline">gasoline</a></i>) as did certain automotive terms (<i><a href="/wiki/Truck" title="Truck">truck</a></i>, <i><a href="/wiki/Trunk_(car)" title="Trunk (car)">trunk</a></i>).
</p><p>New foreign loanwords came with 19th and early 20th century European immigration to the U.S.; notably, from <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Yiddish_language" title="Yiddish language">Yiddish</a> <i>(<a href="/wiki/Chutzpah" title="Chutzpah">chutzpah</a>, schmooze</i>) and <a href="/wiki/German_language" title="German language">German</a> (<i><a href="/wiki/Hamburger" title="Hamburger">hamburger</a>, <a href="/wiki/Hot_dog" title="Hot dog">wiener</a></i>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-70"><a href="#cite_note-70">[69]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-71"><a href="#cite_note-71">[70]</a></sup> A large number of English colloquialisms from various periods are American in origin; some have lost their American flavor (from <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Okay" title="Okay">OK</a></i> and <i>cool</i> to <i><a href="/wiki/Nerd" title="Nerd">nerd</a></i> and <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/24/7" title="24/7">24/7</a>),</i> while others have not <i>(<a href="/wiki/Have_a_nice_day" title="Have a nice day">have a nice day</a>, for sure);</i><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-72"><a href="#cite_note-72">[71]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-73"><a href="#cite_note-73">[72]</a></sup> many are now distinctly old-fashioned <i>(swell, groovy).</i> Some English words now in general use, such as <i>hijacking, <a href="/wiki/Disc_jockey" title="Disc jockey">disc jockey</a>, boost, bulldoze</i> and <i><a href="/wiki/Jazz" title="Jazz">jazz</a>,</i> originated as American slang.
</p><p>American English has always shown a marked tendency <a href="/wiki/Functional_shift" title="Functional shift">to use nouns as verbs</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-iqiyyl_74-0"><a href="#cite_note-iqiyyl-74">[73]</a></sup> Examples of nouns that are now also verbs are <i>interview, advocate, vacuum, lobby, pressure, rear-end, transition, feature, profile, spearhead, skyrocket, showcase, bad-mouth, <a href="/wiki/Vacation" title="Vacation">vacation</a>, major,</i> and many others. <a href="/wiki/Compound_(linguistics)" title="Compound (linguistics)">Compounds</a> coined in the U.S. are for instance <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Foothill" title="Foothill">foothill</a>, <a href="/wiki/Landslide" title="Landslide">landslide</a></i> (in all senses), <i><a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/backdrop" title="wikt:backdrop">backdrop</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Teenager" title="Teenager">teenager</a>,</i>
<a href="/wiki/Brainstorming" title="Brainstorming">brainstorm</a>, <a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/bandwagon" title="wikt:bandwagon">bandwagon</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hitchhike" title="Hitchhike">hitchhike</a>, smalltime, and a huge number of others. Some are euphemistic <i>(<a href="/wiki/Human_resources" title="Human resources">human resources</a>, <a href="/wiki/Affirmative_action" title="Affirmative action">affirmative action</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Correctional_facility" title="Correctional facility">correctional facility</a>).</i> Many compound nouns have the verb-and-preposition combination: <i>stopover, lineup, tryout, spin-off, <a href="/wiki/Shootout" title="Shootout">shootout</a>, holdup, hideout, comeback, <a href="/wiki/Makeover" title="Makeover">makeover</a>,</i> and many more. Some prepositional and phrasal verbs are in fact of American origin (<i>win out, hold up, back up/off/down/out, face up to</i> and many others).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-75"><a href="#cite_note-75">[74]</a></sup>
</p><p>Noun endings such as <i>-ee (retiree), -ery (bakery), -ster (gangster)</i> and <i>-cian (beautician)</i> are also particularly productive in the U.S.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-iqiyyl_74-1"><a href="#cite_note-iqiyyl-74">[73]</a></sup> Several verbs ending in <i>-ize</i> are of U.S. origin; for example, <i>fetishize, prioritize, burglarize, accessorize, <a href="/wiki/Weatherization" title="Weatherization">weatherize</a>,</i> etc; and so are some <a href="/wiki/Back-formation" title="Back-formation">back-formations</a> <i>(locate, fine-tune, curate, donate, emote, upholster</i> and <i>enthuse).</i> Among syntactical constructions that arose are <i>outside of, headed for, meet up with, back of,</i> etc. Americanisms formed by alteration of some existing words include notably <i>pesky, phony, rambunctious, buddy, <a href="/wiki/Sundae" title="Sundae">sundae</a>, skeeter, sashay</i> and <i>kitty-corner.</i> Adjectives that arose in the U.S. are, for example, <i>lengthy, bossy, <a href="/wiki/Cuteness" title="Cuteness">cute</a></i> and <i>cutesy, punk</i> (in all senses), <i>sticky</i> (of the weather), <i>through</i> (as in "finished"), and many colloquial forms such as <i>peppy</i> or <i>wacky</i>.
</p><p>A number of words and meanings that originated in <a href="/wiki/Middle_English" title="Middle English">Middle English</a> or <a href="/wiki/Early_Modern_English" title="Early Modern English">Early Modern English</a> and that have been in everyday use in the United States have since disappeared in most varieties of British English; some of these have cognates in <a href="/wiki/Scottish_Lowlands" title="Scottish Lowlands">Lowland Scots</a>. Terms such as <i><a href="/wiki/Autumn" title="Autumn">fall</a></i> ("autumn"), <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Faucet" title="Faucet">faucet</a></i> ("tap"), <i><a href="/wiki/Diaper" title="Diaper">diaper</a></i> ("nappy"), <i><a href="/wiki/Candy" title="Candy">candy</a></i> ("sweets"), <i><a href="/wiki/Frying_pan" title="Frying pan">skillet</a></i>, <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Eyeglasses" title="Eyeglasses">eyeglasses</a></i>, and <i><a href="/wiki/Obligate" title="Obligate">obligate</a></i> are often regarded as Americanisms. <i>Fall</i> for example came to denote the season in 16th century England, a contraction of Middle English expressions like "fall of the leaf" and "fall of the year".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-76"><a href="#cite_note-76">[75]</a></sup> <i>Gotten</i> (<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Past_participle" title="Past participle">past participle</a> of <i>get</i>) is often considered to be largely an Americanism.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-77"><a href="#cite_note-77">[76]</a></sup> Other words and meanings were brought back to Britain from the U.S., especially in the second half of the 20th century; these include <i>hire</i> ("to employ"), <i>I guess</i> (famously criticized by <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/H._W._Fowler" title="H. W. Fowler">H. W. Fowler</a>), <i><a href="/wiki/Baggage" title="Baggage">baggage</a></i>, <i>hit</i> (a place), and the adverbs <i>overly</i> and <i>presently</i> ("currently"). Some of these, for example, <i><a href="/wiki/Monkey_wrench" title="Monkey wrench">monkey wrench</a></i> and <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Wastebasket" title="Wastebasket">wastebasket</a></i>, originated in 19th century Britain. The adjectives <i>mad</i> meaning "angry", <i>smart</i> meaning "intelligent", and <i>sick</i> meaning "ill" are also more frequent in American (and Irish) English than British English.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-78"><a href="#cite_note-78">[77]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-79"><a href="#cite_note-79">[78]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-80"><a href="#cite_note-80">[79]</a></sup>
</p><p>Linguist <a href="/wiki/Bert_Vaux" title="Bert Vaux">Bert Vaux</a> created a survey, completed in 2003, polling English speakers across the United States about their specific everyday word choices, hoping to identify regionalisms.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-81"><a href="#cite_note-81">[80]</a></sup> The study found that most Americans prefer the term <i>sub</i> for a long sandwich, <i>soda</i> (but <i>pop</i> in the Great Lakes region and generic <i>coke</i> in the South) for a sweet and bubbly <a href="/wiki/Soft_drink" title="Soft drink">soft drink</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-82"><a href="#cite_note-82">[81]</a></sup> <i>you</i> or <i>you guys</i> for the plural of <i>you</i> (but <i>y'all</i> in the South), <i><a href="/wiki/Sneakers" title="Sneakers">sneakers</a></i> for athletic shoes (but often <i>tennis shoes</i> outside the Northeast), and <i><a href="/wiki/Shopping_cart" title="Shopping cart">shopping cart</a></i> for a cart used for carrying supermarket goods.
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Differences_between_British_and_American_English">Differences between British and American English</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=American_English&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: Differences between British and American English">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Comparison_of_American_and_British_English" title="Comparison of American and British English">Comparison of American and British English</a></div>
<p>American English and <a href="/wiki/British_English" title="British English">British English</a> (BrE) often differ at the levels of phonology, phonetics, vocabulary, and, to a much lesser extent, grammar and orthography.
The first large American dictionary, <i>An American Dictionary of the English Language</i>, known as <a href="/wiki/Webster%27s_Dictionary" title="Webster's Dictionary">Webster's Dictionary</a>, was written by <a href="/wiki/Noah_Webster" title="Noah Webster">Noah Webster</a> in 1828, codifying several of these spellings.
</p><p>Differences in grammar are relatively minor, and do not normally affect mutual intelligibility; these include: different use of some <a href="/wiki/Auxiliary_verb" title="Auxiliary verb">auxiliary verbs</a>; formal (rather than notional) agreement with <a href="/wiki/Collective_noun" title="Collective noun">collective nouns</a>; different preferences for the past forms of a few verbs (for example, AmE/BrE: <i>learned</i>/<i>learnt</i>, <i>burned</i>/<i>burnt</i>, <i>snuck/sneaked</i>, <i>dove/dived</i>) although the purportedly "British" forms can occasionally be seen in American English writing as well; different prepositions and adverbs in certain contexts (for example, AmE <i>in school,</i> BrE <i>at school</i>); and whether or not a definite article is used, in very few cases (AmE <i>to the hospital</i>, BrE <i>to hospital</i>; contrast, however, AmE <i>actress Elizabeth Taylor</i>, BrE <i>the actress Elizabeth Taylor</i>). Often, these differences are a matter of relative preferences rather than absolute rules; and most are not stable, since the two varieties are constantly influencing each other,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-83"><a href="#cite_note-83">[82]</a></sup> and American English is not a standardized set of dialects.
</p><p>Differences in <a href="/wiki/Orthography" title="Orthography">orthography</a> are also minor. The main differences are that American English usually uses spellings such as <i>flavor</i> for British <i>flavour</i>, <i>fiber</i> for <i>fibre</i>, <i>defense</i> for <i>defence</i>, <i>analyze</i> for <i>analyse</i>, <i>license</i> for <i>licence</i>, <i>catalog</i> for <i>catalogue</i> and <i>traveling</i> for <i>travelling</i>. Noah Webster popularized such spellings in America, but he did not invent most of them. Rather, "he chose already existing options [...] on such grounds as simplicity, analogy or etymology".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-84"><a href="#cite_note-84">[83]</a></sup> Other differences are due to the <a href="/wiki/Francophile" title="Francophile">francophile</a> tastes of the 19th century <a href="/wiki/Victorian_era" title="Victorian era">Victorian era</a> Britain (for example they preferred <i>programme</i> for <i>program</i>, <i>manoeuvre</i> for <i>maneuver</i>, <i>cheque</i> for <i>check</i>, etc.).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-85"><a href="#cite_note-85">[84]</a></sup> AmE almost always uses <i>-ize</i> in words like <i>realize</i>. BrE prefers <i>-ise</i>, but also uses <i>-ize</i> on occasion (see <a href="/wiki/Oxford_spelling" title="Oxford spelling">Oxford spelling</a>).
</p><p>There are a few differences in punctuation rules. British English is more tolerant of run-on sentences, called "comma splices" in American English, and American English requires that periods and commas be placed inside closing quotation marks even in cases in which British rules would place them outside. American English also favors the double quotation mark over single.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-APA_86-0"><a href="#cite_note-APA-86">[85]</a></sup>
</p><p>AmE sometimes favors words that are <a href="/wiki/Morphology_(linguistics)" title="Morphology (linguistics)">morphologically</a> more complex, whereas BrE uses clipped forms, such as AmE <i>transportation</i> and BrE <i>transport</i> or where the British form is a <a href="/wiki/Back-formation" title="Back-formation">back-formation</a>, such as AmE <i>burglarize</i> and BrE <i>burgle</i> (from <i>burglar</i>). However, while individuals usually use one or the other, both forms will be widely understood and mostly used alongside each other within the two systems.
</p><p>British English also differs from American English in that "schedule" can be pronounced with either [sk] or [ʃ].<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-87"><a href="#cite_note-87">[86]</a></sup>
</p>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Notes">Notes</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=American_English&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: Notes">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="reflist" style="list-style-type: decimal;">
<div class="mw-references-wrap"><ol class="references">
<li id="cite_note-18"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-18">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Dialects are considered "<a href="/wiki/Rhoticity_in_English" title="Rhoticity in English">rhotic</a>" if they pronounce the <i>r</i> sound in all historical environments, without ever "dropping" this sound. The <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Father%E2%80%93bother_merger" title="Father–bother merger"><i>father–bother</i> merger</a> is the pronunciation of the unrounded <span class="nowrap"><span class="IPA nopopups noexcerpt"><a href="/wiki/Help:IPA/English" title="Help:IPA/English">/<span style="border-bottom:1px dotted"><span title="/ɒ/: 'o' in 'body'">ɒ</span></span>/</a></span></span> vowel variant (as in <i>cot, lot, bother</i>, etc.) the same as the <span class="nowrap"><span class="IPA nopopups noexcerpt"><a href="/wiki/Help:IPA/English" title="Help:IPA/English">/<span style="border-bottom:1px dotted"><span title="/ɑː/: 'a' in 'father'">ɑː</span></span>/</a></span></span> vowel (as in <i>spa, haha, Ma</i>), causing words like <i>con</i> and <i>Kahn</i> and like <i>sob</i> and <i><a href="/wiki/Saab_Automobile" title="Saab Automobile">Saab</a></i> to <a href="/wiki/Homophone" title="Homophone">sound identical</a>, with the vowel usually realized in the back or middle of the mouth as <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">[ɑ~ä]</span>. Finally, most of the U.S. participates in a continuous nasal system of the "short <i>a</i>" vowel (in <i>cat, trap, bath</i>, etc.), causing <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">/æ/</span> to be pronounced with the tongue raised and with a glide quality (typically sounding like <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">[ɛə]</span>) particularly when before a <a href="/wiki/Nasal_consonant" title="Nasal consonant">nasal consonant</a>; thus, <i>mad</i> is <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">[mæd]</span>, but <i>man</i> is more like <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">[mɛən]</span>.</span>
</li>
</ol></div></div>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="References">References</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=American_English&amp;action=edit&amp;section=14" title="Edit section: References">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="reflist" style="list-style-type: decimal;">
<div class="mw-references-wrap mw-references-columns"><ol class="references">
<li id="cite_note-ethn-1"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-ethn_1-0">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><a class="external text" href="https://www.ethnologue.com/18/language/eng/" rel="nofollow">English (United States)</a> at <i><a href="/wiki/Ethnologue" title="Ethnologue">Ethnologue</a></i> (18th ed., 2015)</span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-braille-2"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-braille_2-0">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><cite class="citation web"><a class="external text" href="http://www.brailleauthority.org/ueb.html" rel="nofollow">"Unified English Braille (UEB)"</a>. <i>Braille Authority of North America (BANA)</i>. 2 November 2016<span class="reference-accessdate">. <li id="cite_note-3"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-3">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><code>en-US</code> is the <a href="/wiki/Language_code" title="Language code">language code</a> for <i>U.S. English</i>, as defined by <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/ISO_standard" title="ISO standard">ISO standards</a> (see <a href="/wiki/ISO_639-1" title="ISO 639-1">ISO 639-1</a> and <a href="/wiki/ISO_3166-1_alpha-2" title="ISO 3166-1 alpha-2">ISO 3166-1 alpha-2</a>) and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Internet_standard" title="Internet standard">Internet standards</a> (see <a href="/wiki/IETF_language_tag" title="IETF language tag">IETF language tag</a>).</span>

<li id="cite_note-42"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-42">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><i><a href="/wiki/North_American_English" title="North American English">North American English</a></i> (Trudgill, p. 2) is a collective term used for the varieties of the English language that are spoken in both the United States and <a href="/wiki/Canada" title="Canada">Canada</a>.</span>

<li id="cite_note-69"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-69">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">A few of these are now chiefly found, or have been more productive, outside the U.S.; for example, <i>jump,</i> "to drive past a traffic signal"; <i>block</i> meaning "building", and <i>center,</i> "central point in a town" or "main area for a particular activity" (cf. Oxford English Dictionary).</span>

L.</a> (1977) [1921]. <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/The_American_Language:_An_Inquiry_into_the_Development_of_English_in_the_United_States" title="The American Language: An Inquiry into the Development of English in the United States">The American Language: An Inquiry into the Development of English in the United States</a></i> (4th ed.). New York: Knopf.</cite><span class="Z3988" title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Abook&amp;rft.genre=book&amp;rft.btitle=The+American+Language%3A+An+Inquiry+into+the+Development+of+English+in+the+United+States&amp;rft.place=New+York&amp;rft.edition=4th&amp;rft.pub=Knopf&amp;rft.date=1977&amp;rft.aulast=Mencken&amp;rft.aufirst=H.+L.&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3AAmerican+English"></span><link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r861714446" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/></li></ul>
